Switching to a color map:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.cm }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ cm  }\CommentTok{\# We add the import of the cm module (color maps)}

\NormalTok{categories }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Rent\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Food\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Transport\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Entertainment\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Savings\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Other\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{expenses }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1500}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{800}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{400}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{300}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{500}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{250}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\# We use the qualitative color map \textquotesingle{}Set3\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{cmap }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.colormaps[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Set3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [cmap(i) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(categories))]}

\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.pie(expenses, labels}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{categories, colors}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{colors, autopct}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\%1.1f\%\%}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, startangle}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Breakdown of expenses in the household budget\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.axis(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}equal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\NormalTok{colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [cmap(i) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(categories))]}

\NormalTok{fig, ax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{))}

\section{Donut chart}\label{donut-chart}

\protect\phantomsection\label{annotated-cell-193}%
\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\NormalTok{np.random.seed(}\DecValTok{345}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{1}}
\NormalTok{data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.randint(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{2}}
\NormalTok{total }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(data) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{3}}
\NormalTok{data\_per }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ data }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ total }\OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{100} \hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{4}}
\NormalTok{explode }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{5}}
\NormalTok{plt.pie(data\_per, explode}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{explode, labels}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\BuiltInTok{round}\NormalTok{(i, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(data\_per)]) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{6}}
\NormalTok{circle }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.Circle((}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{), }\FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}white\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{7}}
\NormalTok{p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.gcf() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{8}}
\NormalTok{p.gca().add\_artist(circle) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{9}}
\NormalTok{plt.show() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{10}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{description}
\tightlist
\item[\circled{1}]
\texttt{np.random.seed(345)}: sets the seed of the random number
generator to the value 345, which ensures the reproducibility of the
results.
\item[\circled{2}]
\texttt{data\ =\ np.random.randint(20,\ 100,\ 6)}: generates an array of
6 random integers in the range from 20 to 99 (inclusive).
\item[\circled{3}]
\texttt{total\ =\ sum(data)}: calculates the sum of all the generated
numbers.
\item[\circled{4}]
\texttt{data\_per\ =\ data\ /\ total\ *\ 100}: calculates the percentage
values of each number relative to the total sum.
\item[\circled{5}]
\texttt{explode\ =\ (0.2,\ 0,\ 0,\ 0,\ 0,\ 0)}: creates a tuple defining
the slice offset for each element (the first slice will be offset by
0.2).
\item[\circled{6}]
\texttt{plt.pie(data\_per,\ explode=explode,\ labels={[}round(i,\ 2)\ for\ i\ in\ list(data\_per){]})}:
creates a pie chart with the percentage values, with the specified
offset and labels rounded to 2 decimal places.
\item[\circled{7}]
\texttt{circle\ =\ plt.Circle((0,\ 0),\ 0.7,\ color=\textquotesingle{}white\textquotesingle{})}:
creates a white circle centered at the point (0, 0) with a radius of
0.7.
\item[\circled{8}]
\texttt{p\ =\ plt.gcf()}: gets the current figure object (get current
figure).
\item[\circled{9}]
\texttt{p.gca().add\_artist(circle)}: adds the created white circle to
the current chart axes, creating a ``donut chart'' effect.
\item[\circled{10}]
\texttt{plt.show()}: displays the chart.
\end{description}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-piechart_files/figure-pdf/cell-10-output-1.pdf}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\NormalTok{np.random.seed(}\DecValTok{345}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.randint(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{total }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(data)}
\NormalTok{data\_per }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ data }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ total }\OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{100}
\NormalTok{explode }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{fig, ax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots()}
\NormalTok{ax.pie(data\_per, explode}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{explode, labels}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\BuiltInTok{round}\NormalTok{(i, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(data\_per)])}
\NormalTok{circle }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.Circle((}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{), }\FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}white\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax.add\_artist(circle)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-piechart_files/figure-pdf/cell-11-output-1.pdf}}

\chapter{Matplotlib - bar chart}\label{matplotlib---bar-chart}

A bar chart is used to present categorical or discrete data. It is a
commonly used type of chart that helps to visually compare values or
quantities for different categories. Here are a few types of data for
which a bar chart can be used:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Frequencies: A bar chart is used to present the number of occurrences
  of different categories, such as survey results, consumer preferences,
  or different population groups.
\item
  Proportions: It can be used to present the percentage share of
  individual categories in the whole, e.g.~the market share of different
  companies, percentage test results, or the percentage distribution of
  the population by age.
\item
  Numeric values: A bar chart can present numeric values associated with
  different categories, e.g.~product sales, revenue from different
  sources, or the average temperature in different cities.
\item
  Time series data: A bar chart can also be used to present time series
  data when changes occur at regular time intervals, e.g.~annual sales,
  monthly rainfall, or weekly revenue.
\end{enumerate}

It is worth noting that bar charts are appropriate when dealing with a
small number of categories, because too many bars on a chart can make it
difficult to interpret. In such cases, it is worth considering other
types of charts, such as a pie chart or a proportional chart.

The \texttt{bar} function in the Matplotlib library is used to create
bar charts. Bar charts are often used when we want to compare values of
different categories.

The function syntax is
\texttt{plt.bar(x,\ height,\ width=0.8,\ bottom=None,\ align=\textquotesingle{}center\textquotesingle{},\ data=None,\ **kwargs)},
where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{x} - positions of the bars on the X axis. This can be a
  sequence of numeric values or a list of labels that will be placed on
  the X axis.
\item
  \texttt{height} - the height of the bars.
\item
  \texttt{width} - the width of the bars.
\item
  \texttt{bottom} - the position of the bottom edge of the bars. Set to
  \texttt{None} by default, which means the bars start from zero.
\item
  \texttt{align} - how the bars are centered along the X axis. Set to
  `center' by default.
\item
  \texttt{data} - a DataFrame object that contains the data for the
  chart.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for chart formatting, such as
  color, transparency, axis labels, title, and legend.
\end{itemize}

\protect\phantomsection\label{annotated-cell-195}%
\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{1}}

\NormalTok{categories }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{2}}
\NormalTok{values }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{] }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{3}}

\NormalTok{plt.bar(categories, values, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{4}}

\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Bar chart\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{5}}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Categories\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{6}}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Values\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{7}}

\NormalTok{plt.show() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{8}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{description}
\tightlist
\item[\circled{1}]
\texttt{import\ matplotlib.pyplot\ as\ plt}: imports the
matplotlib.pyplot library as \texttt{plt}, which is needed to create
charts.
\item[\circled{2}]
\texttt{categories\ =\ {[}\textquotesingle{}Category\ 1\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}Category\ 2\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}Category\ 3\textquotesingle{}{]}}:
creates a list \texttt{categories} containing the names of three
categories that will be used on the X axis of the bar chart.
\item[\circled{3}]
\texttt{values\ =\ {[}10,\ 20,\ 15{]}}: creates a list \texttt{values}
containing the numeric values corresponding to each category - these
values determine the height of the individual bars.
\item[\circled{4}]
\texttt{plt.bar(categories,\ values,\ color=\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{},\ alpha=0.5)}:
creates a bar chart, where:
\item[\circled{5}]
\texttt{plt.title(\textquotesingle{}Bar\ chart\textquotesingle{})}:
gives the chart the title ``Bar chart''.
\item[\circled{6}]
\texttt{plt.xlabel(\textquotesingle{}Categories\textquotesingle{})}:
adds an X axis label with the text ``Categories''.
\item[\circled{7}]
\texttt{plt.ylabel(\textquotesingle{}Values\textquotesingle{})}: adds a
Y axis label with the text ``Values''.
\item[\circled{8}]
\texttt{plt.show()}: displays the prepared chart.
\end{description}

    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q4\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}

    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{23}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{51}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{],}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{22}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{quarters }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{8}}
\NormalTok{X }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(quarters)) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{9}}
\NormalTok{plt.bar(X }\OperatorTok{{-}} \FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, phones, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}dodgerblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, width}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{10}}
\NormalTok{plt.bar(X, laptops, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mediumseagreen\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, width}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{11}}
\NormalTok{plt.bar(X }\OperatorTok{+} \FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, tablets, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}tomato\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, width}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{12}}
\NormalTok{plt.xticks(X, quarters) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{13}}
\NormalTok{plt.legend() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{14}}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Sales of electronic products in individual quarters\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{15}}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Number of units sold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{16}}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{17}}
\NormalTok{plt.grid(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{18}}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{19}}
\NormalTok{plt.show() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{20}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{description}
\tightlist
\item[\circled{1}]
\texttt{import\ numpy\ as\ np}: imports the NumPy library for numerical
operations and assigns it the alias \texttt{np}.
\item[\circled{2}]
\texttt{import\ matplotlib.pyplot\ as\ plt}: imports the pyplot module
from the Matplotlib library for creating charts and assigns it the alias
\texttt{plt}.
\item[\circled{3}]
\texttt{import\ pandas\ as\ pd}: imports the Pandas library for working
with data and assigns it the alias \texttt{pd}.
\item[\circled{4}]
\texttt{df\ =\ pd.DataFrame(\{...\})}: creates a DataFrame with data on
the sales of electronic products in individual quarters.
\item[\circled{5}]
\texttt{phones\ =\ df{[}\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{}{]}}:
extracts the `Phones' column from the DataFrame and assigns it to the
variable \texttt{phones}.
\item[\circled{6}]
\texttt{laptops\ =\ df{[}\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}{]}}:
extracts the `Laptops' column from the DataFrame and assigns it to the
variable \texttt{laptops}.
\item[\circled{7}]
\texttt{tablets\ =\ df{[}\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}{]}}:
extracts the `Tablets' column from the DataFrame and assigns it to the
variable \texttt{tablets}.
\item[\circled{8}]
\texttt{quarters\ =\ df{[}\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}{]}}:
extracts the `Quarter' column from the DataFrame and assigns it to the
variable \texttt{quarters}.
\item[\circled{9}]
\texttt{X\ =\ np.arange(len(quarters))}: creates an array of indices
from 0 to the length of the quarters list (0, 1, 2, 3), which will be
used to position the bars.
\item[\circled{10}]
\texttt{plt.bar(X\ -\ 0.25,\ phones,\ color=\textquotesingle{}dodgerblue\textquotesingle{},\ width=0.25,\ label=\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{})}:
draws the bars for phones shifted to the left (-0.25), in blue and with
a width of 0.25.
\item[\circled{11}]
\texttt{plt.bar(X,\ laptops,\ color=\textquotesingle{}mediumseagreen\textquotesingle{},\ width=0.25,\ label=\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{})}:
draws the bars for laptops in the center (no shift), in green and with a
width of 0.25.
\item[\circled{12}]
\texttt{plt.bar(X\ +\ 0.25,\ tablets,\ color=\textquotesingle{}tomato\textquotesingle{},\ width=0.25,\ label=\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{})}:
draws the bars for tablets shifted to the right (+0.25), in red and with
a width of 0.25.
\item[\circled{13}]
\texttt{plt.xticks(X,\ quarters)}: sets the X axis labels to the names
of the quarters (Q1, Q2, Q3, Q4) at the positions defined by the array
X.
\item[\circled{14}]
\texttt{plt.legend()}: adds a legend to the chart that shows the colors
and names of the individual product categories.
\item[\circled{15}]
\texttt{plt.title(\textquotesingle{}Sales\ of\ electronic\ products\ in\ individual\ quarters\textquotesingle{},\ fontsize=14)}:
sets the chart title with a font size of 14.
\item[\circled{16}]
\texttt{plt.ylabel(\textquotesingle{}Number\ of\ units\ sold\textquotesingle{})}:
adds a Y axis label describing what the values on the vertical axis
represent.
\item[\circled{17}]
\texttt{plt.xlabel(\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{})}: adds
an X axis label describing what the values on the horizontal axis
represent.
\item[\circled{18}]
\texttt{plt.grid(axis=\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{},\ alpha=0.3)}:
adds horizontal grid lines (only on the Y axis) with a transparency of
0.3.
\item[\circled{19}]
\texttt{plt.tight\_layout()}: automatically adjusts the chart margins so
that all elements are clearly visible and do not overlap.
\item[\circled{20}]
\texttt{plt.show()}: displays the chart
\end{description}

\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{1}}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{2}}

\NormalTok{N }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{5} \hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{3}}

\NormalTok{boys }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{27}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{4}}
\NormalTok{girls }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{32}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{34}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{5}}
\NormalTok{ind }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(N) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{6}}
\NormalTok{width }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.35} \hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{7}}

\NormalTok{plt.bar(ind, boys, width, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"boys"}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{8}}
\NormalTok{plt.bar(ind, girls, width, bottom}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{boys, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"girls"}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{9}}

\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Contribution\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{10}}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Contribution by the teams\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{11}}
\NormalTok{plt.xticks(ind, (}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}T1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}T2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}T3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}T4\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}T5\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{12}}
\NormalTok{plt.yticks(np.arange(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{81}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{13}}
\NormalTok{plt.legend() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{14}}
\NormalTok{plt.show() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{15}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{description}
\tightlist
\item[\circled{1}]
\texttt{import\ numpy\ as\ np}: imports the NumPy library for numerical
operations and assigns it the alias \texttt{np}.
\item[\circled{2}]
\texttt{import\ matplotlib.pyplot\ as\ plt}: imports the pyplot module
from the Matplotlib library for creating charts and assigns it the alias
\texttt{plt}.
\item[\circled{3}]
\texttt{N\ =\ 5}: defines a constant N equal to 5, which represents the
number of teams/data groups.
\item[\circled{4}]
\texttt{boys\ =\ (20,\ 35,\ 30,\ 35,\ 27)}: creates a tuple containing
the contribution values of the boys for each of the 5 teams.
\item[\circled{5}]
\texttt{girls\ =\ (25,\ 32,\ 34,\ 20,\ 25)}: creates a tuple containing
the contribution values of the girls for each of the 5 teams.
\item[\circled{6}]
\texttt{ind\ =\ np.arange(N)}: creates an array of indices {[}0, 1, 2,
3, 4{]}, which defines the positions of the bars on the X axis.
\item[\circled{7}]
\texttt{width\ =\ 0.35}: sets the width of the bars to 0.35 units.
\item[\circled{8}]
\texttt{plt.bar(ind,\ boys,\ width,\ label="boys")}: draws the bars for
the boys at the positions defined by \texttt{ind}, with heights from
\texttt{boys}, a width of \texttt{width} and the label ``boys''.
\item[\circled{9}]
\texttt{plt.bar(ind,\ girls,\ width,\ bottom=boys,\ label="girls")}:
draws the bars for the girls at the same positions, but using the
\texttt{bottom=boys} parameter, which places the girls' bars on top of
the boys' bars, creating a stacked bar chart.
\item[\circled{10}]
\texttt{plt.ylabel(\textquotesingle{}Contribution\textquotesingle{})}:
adds a Y axis label describing that the values represent contribution.
\item[\circled{11}]
\texttt{plt.title(\textquotesingle{}Contribution\ by\ the\ teams\textquotesingle{})}:
gives the chart the title ``Contribution by the teams''.
\item[\circled{12}]
\texttt{plt.xticks(ind,\ (\textquotesingle{}T1\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}T2\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}T3\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}T4\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}T5\textquotesingle{}))}:
sets the X axis labels to the team names (T1, T2, T3, T4, T5) at the
positions defined by the array \texttt{ind}.
\item[\circled{13}]
\texttt{plt.yticks(np.arange(0,\ 81,\ 10))}: sets the ticks on the Y
axis every 10 units, from 0 to 80.
\item[\circled{14}]
\texttt{plt.legend()}: adds a legend to the chart that shows the colors
and labels for the boys and girls.
\item[\circled{15}]
\texttt{plt.show()}: displays the chart
\end{description}

\NormalTok{N }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{5}
\NormalTok{boys }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{27}\NormalTok{)}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{m9.png}}

The \texttt{barh} function is used to create horizontal bar charts.
Horizontal bar charts are often used when we want to compare values of
different categories and the labels on the X axis are long or very
numerous.

The function syntax is
\texttt{plt.barh(y,\ width,\ height=0.8,\ left=None,\ align=\textquotesingle{}center\textquotesingle{},\ data=None,\ **kwargs)},
where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{y} - positions of the bars on the Y axis. This can be a
  sequence of numeric values or a list of labels that will be placed on
  the Y axis.
\item
  \texttt{width} - the width of the bars.
\item
  \texttt{height} - the height of the bars.
\item
  \texttt{left} - the position of the left edge of the bars. Set to
  \texttt{None} by default, which means the bars start from zero.
\item
  \texttt{align} - how the bars are centered along the Y axis. Set to
  `center' by default.
\item
  \texttt{data} - a DataFrame object that contains the data for the
  chart.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for chart formatting, such as
  color, transparency, axis labels, title, and legend.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\CommentTok{\# Data}
\NormalTok{categories }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Category 3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{values }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\# Creating a horizontal bar chart}
\NormalTok{plt.barh(categories, values, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Adding a title and axis labels}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Horizontal bar chart\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Values\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Categories\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Displaying the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}
\NormalTok{sales\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}product\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Smartphone\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablet\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptop\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Headphones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Smartwatch\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}units\_sold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{156}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{89}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{234}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{312}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{178}\NormalTok{],}

\NormalTok{\}}
\NormalTok{df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(sales\_data)}
\NormalTok{y\_pos }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(df))}
\NormalTok{colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}phones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}computers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
          \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}accessories\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}purple\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}watch\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}orange\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Products\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.grid(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.show()}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}

\NormalTok{df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(\{}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Q4\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{],}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{],}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{23}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{51}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{],}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: [}\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{22}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{\})}

\NormalTok{phones }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{laptops }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{tablets }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{quarters }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ df[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{Y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(quarters))}
\NormalTok{plt.barh(Y, phones, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}dodgerblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, height}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Phones\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.barh(Y }\OperatorTok{+} \FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, laptops, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mediumseagreen\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, height}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Laptops\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.barh(Y }\OperatorTok{+} \FloatTok{0.50}\NormalTok{, tablets, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}tomato\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, height}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Tablets\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.yticks(Y }\OperatorTok{+} \FloatTok{0.25}\NormalTok{, quarters)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Sales of electronic products in individual quarters\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Number of units sold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Quarter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.grid(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{)}

\chapter{Matplotlib - regression}\label{matplotlib---regression}

The following section discusses an example of how polynomial regression
works. Other types of regression can be obtained by choosing a different
command to generate it.

\textbf{Regression} - a statistical method that allows for examining the
relationship between data quantities. The goal of multivariate
regression is to quantitatively capture the relationships between many
independent variables (explanatory variables, factors, predictors) and a
dependent variable (criterion, explained variable, response).

Examples of multivariate regression:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The strength of concrete depends on the components used in its
  production. Question: In what proportion should these components be
  used so that the strength is greatest?
\item
  The price of an apartment depends on\ldots. Question: how does the
  share of individual elements affect the market price so that it is
  highest?
\item
  Granting a loan depends on \ldots. Question: how does the share of
  individual elements affect the decision to grant a loan or not?
  \emph{is this really regression?}
\end{itemize}

In ``scientific'' terms, statistical research is generally meant to
explain the relationships between different features of the studied
population.

The goals of research in regression analysis:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Characterizing the relationship (among other things, its range,
  direction, and strength).
\item
  Determining a mathematical model that most reliably reflects the
  behavior of the response (in other words, finding an appropriate
  function that can later be used for prediction).
\item
  Determining which of the explanatory variables are important in the
  analysis of interdependence and ranking these variables according to
  the strength of their influence on the explained variable.
\item
  Comparing different models for one explained variable, i.e.~comparing
  models that consist of different sets of explanatory variables.
\item
  Determining the interaction of explanatory variables and (in the case
  of a two-way relationship) determining the dependence of the
  explanatory variables on the explained variable.
\item
  Point estimation of the values of the regression coefficients (the
  direction and strength of interdependence as well as the statistical
  significance of the parameters introduced into the model).
\end{itemize}

The obtained results should be verified against the following criteria:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Determining a logical relationship between the variables,
  i.e.~checking whether the obtained results do not conflict with the
  nature of the phenomenon.
\item
  Checking whether the cause precedes the effect in time.
\item
  Analyzing the strength of the relationship between variables, e.g.~a
  high correlation between variables that in reality do not affect each
  other.
\item
  Checking whether the obtained model works in reality.
\item
  Consistency of the results.
\item
  Determining the agreement of the results with theoretical and
  experimental knowledge.
\item
  Considering the possibility of obtaining the studied effect as a
  manifestation of the action of different causes, as well as the
  possibility of several effects arising from one cause.
\end{itemize}

The most commonly used functions in regression analysis:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  linear function \(f(x)=ax+b\),
\item
  polynomial function, e.g.~quadratic \(f(x)=ax^2+bx+c\),
\item
  logarithmic function \(f(x) = \ln x\),
\item
  exponential function \(f(x)=e^{-x}\),
\item
  logistic function \(f(x) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-x}}\).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{\emph{Choosing a method for the interdependence of many
variables:}}

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4194}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4194}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1613}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Analysis method
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Explained variable
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Explanatory variables
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Multiple regression & continuous & continuous (discrete also allowed) \\
Analysis of variance & continuous & qualitative \\
Analysis of covariance & continuous & qualitative (symbolic) and
continuous \\
Poisson regression & discrete & various types \\
Logistic regression & binary & various types \\
\end{longtable}
}

Applications:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Econometrics: Multivariate regression can be used to analyze the
  impact of various factors on economic growth, such as investment,
  consumption, inflation, or the level of employment.
\item
  Medicine: In medical research, multivariate regression can help
  identify factors influencing the development of diseases, such as age,
  diet, lifestyle, or genetic predisposition.
\item
  Marketing: Multivariate regression can be used to analyze the impact
  of various product features on sales, e.g.~prices, advertising, type
  of packaging, or competition.
\item
  Finance: Multivariate regression can be used to analyze the impact of
  various factors on return on investment, such as risk, interest rates,
  economic growth, or fiscal policy.
\item
  Engineering: In engineering, multivariate regression can help analyze
  the impact of various parameters on machine performance, such as
  temperature, pressure, or speed.
\item
  Social sciences: In the social sciences, multivariate regression can
  be used to analyze the impact of various factors on students'
  educational outcomes, such as the parents' level of education, income,
  or cultural environment.
\end{itemize}

We consider the influence of a set of \(k\) variables
\(X_1, \ldots, X_k\) on the variable \(Y\). As many independent
variables as possible should be introduced into the model, and it should
include variables strongly correlated with the dependent variable while
at the same time as weakly correlated with each other as possible.

A linear multivariate regression model:

\[Y=\beta_0 +\beta_1\cdot X_1+\beta_2\cdot X_2+\ldots+\beta_k\cdot X_k+\varepsilon.\]
\(\beta_i\) - regression coefficients (model parameters) describing the
influence of the \(i\)-th variable. \(\varepsilon\) - the random
component.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\CommentTok{\# Example data}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\FloatTok{2.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{2.9}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{4.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{6.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{8.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{9.9}\NormalTok{])}

\CommentTok{\# Fitting a straight line (degree 1) with full results}
\NormalTok{coefs, stats }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.polynomial.polynomial.polyfit(x, y, deg}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, full}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Displaying the results}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Fit coefficients:"}\NormalTok{, coefs)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\StringTok{Diagnostic information:"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Residuals:"}\NormalTok{, stats[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])  }\CommentTok{\# Sum of squared residuals}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Rank of the system matrix:"}\NormalTok{, stats[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Singular values of the system matrix:"}\NormalTok{, stats[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{])}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Estimated fit error:"}\NormalTok{, stats[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
Fit coefficients: [1.51428571 1.61428571]

Diagnostic information:
Residuals: [0.87142857]
Rank of the system matrix: 2
Singular values of the system matrix: [1.35119311 0.41746518]
Estimated fit error: 1.3322676295501878e-15
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\CommentTok{\# Example data}
\NormalTok{np.random.seed(}\DecValTok{42}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Setting the random seed for reproducibility of results}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.linspace(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# 20 evenly spaced points between 0 and 10}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{3} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ x }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{7} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ np.random.normal(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(x))  }\CommentTok{\# y data with random noise}

\CommentTok{\# Fitting a straight line (linear regression)}
\NormalTok{coefs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.polynomial.polynomial.polyfit(x, y, deg}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Regression coefficients (y = a + b*x)}

\CommentTok{\# Determining the regression line}
\NormalTok{x\_fit }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.linspace(}\BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(x), }\BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(x), }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Additional points for a smooth regression line}
\NormalTok{y\_fit }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ coefs[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ coefs[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ x\_fit}

\CommentTok{\# Creating the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(x, y, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Data points\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Scatter plot}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(x\_fit, y\_fit, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\SpecialStringTok{f\textquotesingle{}Regression line\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Regression line}

\CommentTok{\# Adjusting the appearance of the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Linear regression using numpy.polyfit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.grid(}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.6}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.tight\_layout()}

\CommentTok{\# Displaying the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\CommentTok{\# Example data}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{])  }\CommentTok{\# x points}

\protect\phantomsection\label{annotated-cell-210}%
\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{z }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(x, y, c}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{z, cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}viridis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{1}}
\NormalTok{plt.colorbar() }\hspace*{\fill}\NormalTok{\circled{2}}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}X axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Y axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Color map for a scatter plot\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{description}
\tightlist
\item[\circled{1}]
\texttt{plt.scatter(x,\ y,\ c=z,\ cmap=\textquotesingle{}viridis\textquotesingle{})}:
this line creates a scatter plot with the data \texttt{x}, \texttt{y}
and \texttt{z}. \texttt{x} and \texttt{y} are the data that will be
displayed on the X and Y axes, while \texttt{z} is the data that will be
used to create the color map. The
\texttt{cmap=\textquotesingle{}viridis\textquotesingle{}} argument
specifies the color map that will be used to assign colors to the
numeric values.
\item[\circled{2}]
\texttt{plt.colorbar()}: this line adds a colorbar to the scatter plot.
The colorbar indicates which colors correspond to the numeric values on
the color map.
\end{description}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-colors_files/figure-pdf/cell-2-output-1.pdf}}

Color maps

A list of built-in color maps:
\url{https://matplotlib.org/stable/tutorials/colors/colormaps.html}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.colors }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ Normalize}

\CommentTok{\# Example data}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{z }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{100}

\CommentTok{\# Creating the color map}
\NormalTok{norm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Normalize(vmin}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, vmax}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cmap }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.cm.viridis}

\CommentTok{\# Creating a scatter plot with a color map}
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(x, y, c}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{z, cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{cmap, norm}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{norm)}
\NormalTok{plt.colorbar()}

\CommentTok{\# Adding axis labels}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}X axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Y axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Color map for a scatter plot\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Displaying the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-colors_files/figure-pdf/cell-3-output-1.pdf}}

\chapter{Matplotlib - chart options}\label{matplotlib---chart-options}

\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{figure} arguments in
Matplotlib}{figure arguments in Matplotlib}}\label{figure-arguments-in-matplotlib}

In Matplotlib, a key role in managing chart properties is played by the
\textbf{figure} object, which represents the ``canvas'' on which all
graphical elements are drawn. The \texttt{plt.figure()} and
\texttt{plt.subplots()} functions allow you to customize various aspects
of the figure.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.figure(num}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, dpi}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, facecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, edgecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, frameon}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, tight\_layout}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Arguments:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1206}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.7163}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1631}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Argument
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Description
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Default value
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textbf{\texttt{num}} & Identification number or name of the figure (can
be a number or a string). & \texttt{None} (automatic) \\
\textbf{\texttt{figsize}} & Size of the figure in inches, given as a
tuple \texttt{(width,\ height)}. & \texttt{(6.4,\ 4.8)} \\
\textbf{\texttt{dpi}} & Resolution of the figure in dots per inch. &
\texttt{100} \\
\textbf{\texttt{facecolor}} & Background color of the figure (the whole
canvas). & \texttt{white} \\
\textbf{\texttt{edgecolor}} & Color of the figure's border. &
\texttt{white} \\
\textbf{\texttt{frameon}} & Whether the figure should have a frame
(\texttt{True} or \texttt{False}). & \texttt{True} \\
\textbf{\texttt{tight\_layout}} & Automatic adjustment of elements on
the figure to avoid overlapping (\texttt{True} or \texttt{False}). &
\texttt{False} \\
\end{longtable}
}

\NormalTok{plt.plot([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{])}

\section{Styles}\label{styles}

Matplotlib offers built-in styles that allow for quick and easy
customization of the appearance of charts. Using the
\texttt{plt.style.use} function, you can load a ready-made style, so
that the charts gain a consistent and aesthetic appearance without the
need to manually set all the parameters.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.style.use(style)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\CommentTok{\# Using the \textquotesingle{}ggplot\textquotesingle{} style}
\NormalTok{plt.style.use(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}ggplot\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.plot([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"Chart in the ggplot style"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\chapter{Matplotlib - extras part 2.}\label{matplotlib---extras-part-2.}

\section{Horizontal and vertical
lines}\label{horizontal-and-vertical-lines}

The functions \texttt{axhline} and \texttt{axvline} are used to add
horizontal and vertical lines to a chart, respectively.

\texttt{axhline} draws a horizontal line passing through a specified
value on the Y axis, regardless of the value on the X axis. The function
syntax is \texttt{axhline(y,\ xmin,\ xmax,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{y} - the value on the Y axis through which the line passes
  (default 0)
\item
  \texttt{xmin}, \texttt{xmax} - values in the range 0-1 specifying the
  start and end of the line on the X axis (default 0 and 1)
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments, such as \texttt{color},
  \texttt{linewidth}, or \texttt{linestyle}, used to control the
  appearance of the line
\end{itemize}

\texttt{axvline} draws a vertical line passing through a specified value
on the X axis, regardless of the value on the Y axis. The function
syntax is \texttt{axvline(x,\ ymin,\ ymax,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{x} - the value on the X axis through which the line passes
  (default 0)
\item
  \texttt{ymin}, \texttt{ymax} - values in the range 0-1 specifying the
  start and end of the line on the Y axis (default 0 and 1)
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments, such as \texttt{color},
  \texttt{linewidth}, or \texttt{linestyle}, used to control the
  appearance of the line
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{plt.plot([}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.axhline(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Horizontal line passing through Y=2}
\NormalTok{plt.axvline(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# Vertical line passing through X=3, dashed line style}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-2-output-1.pdf}}

In the example above, \texttt{axhline} draws a red horizontal line
passing through the value 2 on the Y axis, while \texttt{axvline} draws
a green dashed vertical line passing through the value 3 on the X axis.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{x1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ x[x }\OperatorTok{\textless{}} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{y1 }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ x1}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(x1, y1)}
\NormalTok{x2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ x[x }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{y2 }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ x2}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(x2, y2)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylim(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.axhline(y}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"{-}{-}"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.axvline(x}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{":"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-3-output-1.pdf}}

\section{Annotations (text) on a
chart}\label{annotations-text-on-a-chart}

The \texttt{annotate} function is used to add annotations (text and
arrows) to a chart in order to highlight or mark specific points or
areas.

The function syntax is
\texttt{annotate(text,\ xy,\ xytext,\ arrowprops,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{text} - a string representing the annotation text.
\item
  \texttt{xy} - a tuple (x, y) specifying the coordinates of the point
  referred to in the annotation.
\item
  \texttt{xytext} - a tuple (x, y) specifying the coordinates at which
  the annotation text should begin. If not provided, the text will be
  displayed directly at the \texttt{xy} coordinates.
\item
  \texttt{arrowprops} - a dictionary containing options for drawing the
  arrow, such as \texttt{arrowstyle}, \texttt{color}, or
  \texttt{linewidth}. If not provided, the arrow will not be drawn.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for text formatting, such as
  \texttt{fontsize}, \texttt{color}, or \texttt{fontweight}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{plt.plot([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{], marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}X{-}axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Y{-}axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.annotate(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Important point (2, 4)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{             xy}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{),                           }\CommentTok{\# Coordinates of the point to mark}
\NormalTok{             xytext}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{2.5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{),                     }\CommentTok{\# Coordinates of the start of the text}
\NormalTok{             arrowprops}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{dict}\NormalTok{(facecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),    }\CommentTok{\# Arrow properties (color)}
\NormalTok{             fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{,                         }\CommentTok{\# Font size}
\NormalTok{             color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,                        }\CommentTok{\# Text color}
\NormalTok{             fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)                   }\CommentTok{\# Font weight}

\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-4-output-1.pdf}}

If you want to add an annotation with text only, the function syntax is
\texttt{annotate(text,\ xy,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{text} - a string representing the annotation text.
\item
  \texttt{xy} - a tuple (x, y) specifying the coordinates at which the
  annotation text should begin.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for text formatting, such as
  \texttt{fontsize}, \texttt{color}, \texttt{fontweight}, or
  \texttt{horizontalalignment}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{plt.plot([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{], [}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{], marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}X{-}axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Y{-}axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.annotate(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Important point (2, 4)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{             xy}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{),                           }\CommentTok{\# Coordinates of the start of the text}
\NormalTok{             fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{,                         }\CommentTok{\# Font size}
\NormalTok{             color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,                        }\CommentTok{\# Text color}
\NormalTok{             fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,                   }\CommentTok{\# Font weight}
\NormalTok{             horizontalalignment}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}right\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)         }\CommentTok{\# Right{-}align the text}

\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-5-output-1.pdf}}

\section{Axis labels}\label{axis-labels}

The functions \texttt{xlabel} and \texttt{ylabel} are used to add labels
to the X and Y axes on a chart, respectively. Axis labels help to better
understand the presented data by indicating what values are represented
on each axis.

The function syntax is \texttt{xlabel(label,\ **kwargs)} or
\texttt{ylabel(label,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{label} - a string representing the axis label text.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for label formatting, such as
  \texttt{fontsize}, \texttt{color}, \texttt{fontweight}, or
  \texttt{horizontalalignment}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{plt.plot(x, y, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}X axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Y axis\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}green\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, fontweight}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bold\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-6-output-1.pdf}}

The \texttt{annotate} function allows the use of LaTeX syntax in the
annotation text, which is especially useful when we want to add
mathematical equations or symbols to a chart. To use LaTeX syntax, the
text must be placed between dollar signs (\texttt{\$}).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.linspace(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.sin(x)}

\NormalTok{plt.plot(x, y)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sin(x)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Annotation with text in LaTeX syntax}
\NormalTok{plt.annotate(}\VerbatimStringTok{r\textquotesingle{}}\DecValTok{$\textbackslash{}s}\VerbatimStringTok{in}\KeywordTok{(}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}f}\VerbatimStringTok{rac\{2}\ErrorTok{\textbackslash{}}\VerbatimStringTok{pi\}}\OperatorTok{\{3\}}\KeywordTok{)}\VerbatimStringTok{=}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}f}\VerbatimStringTok{rac\{}\DecValTok{\textbackslash{}s}\VerbatimStringTok{qrt}\OperatorTok{\{3\}}\VerbatimStringTok{\}}\OperatorTok{\{2\}}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{             xy}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, np.sqrt(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{/} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{),  }\CommentTok{\# Coordinates of the point to mark}
\NormalTok{             xytext}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{1.0}\NormalTok{),                     }\CommentTok{\# Coordinates of the start of the text}
\NormalTok{             fontsize}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{,                         }\CommentTok{\# Font size}
\NormalTok{             arrowprops}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{dict}\NormalTok{(facecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{),    }\CommentTok{\# Arrow properties (color)}
\NormalTok{             bbox}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{dict}\NormalTok{(facecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}white\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))        }\CommentTok{\# Box around the text (background color)}

\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-extras2_files/figure-pdf/cell-7-output-1.pdf}}

\section{Axis tick labels}\label{axis-tick-labels}

The functions \texttt{xticks} and \texttt{yticks} are used to manipulate
the labels of the X and Y axes and the values on the axis, respectively.
They allow you to control the display of labels, the spacing between
them, and the formatting.

\texttt{xticks} manipulates the labels and values on the X axis, and
\texttt{yticks} on the Y axis. The function syntax is
\texttt{xticks(ticks,\ labels,\ **kwargs)} or
\texttt{yticks(ticks,\ labels,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{ticks} - a list of values for which labels are to be placed on
  the axis. If not provided, the current values remain.
\item
  \texttt{labels} - a list of strings to be used as labels for the
  values from the \texttt{ticks} list. If not provided, the default
  labels will be used.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for label formatting, such as
  \texttt{fontsize}, \texttt{color}, \texttt{fontweight}, or
  \texttt{rotation}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.sin(x)}

\NormalTok{plt.plot(x, y)}

\NormalTok{xtick\_vals }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, np.pi, }\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi, }\DecValTok{3} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi]}
\NormalTok{xtick\_labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\VerbatimStringTok{r\textquotesingle{}}\DecValTok{$}\ErrorTok{\textbackslash{}}\VerbatimStringTok{pi}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\VerbatimStringTok{r\textquotesingle{}}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{2}\ErrorTok{\textbackslash{}}\VerbatimStringTok{pi}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\VerbatimStringTok{r\textquotesingle{}}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{3}\ErrorTok{\textbackslash{}}\VerbatimStringTok{pi}\DecValTok{$}\VerbatimStringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{ytick\_vals }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{ytick\_labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}1/2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}1/2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}

\end{Highlighting}

\section{Grid}\label{grid}

The \texttt{grid} function allows you to add a grid to a chart, which
makes it easier to assess values on the axes and compare them. You can
control the color, width, and style of the grid lines, as well as
specify which axes should have a grid.

The function syntax is
\texttt{plt.grid(b=None,\ which=\textquotesingle{}major\textquotesingle{},\ axis=\textquotesingle{}both\textquotesingle{},\ **kwargs)},
where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{b} - a boolean value (True/False) that determines whether the
  grid is to be displayed. Set to \texttt{None} by default, which means
  that Matplotlib automatically determines whether the grid should be
  displayed based on the configuration.
\item
  \texttt{which} - specifies which grid lines are to be displayed:
  `major' (only grid lines for major ticks), `minor' (grid lines for
  minor ticks), or `both' (default - grid lines for both types of
  ticks).
\item
  \texttt{axis} - specifies which axes should have a grid: `both' (both
  axes), `x' (only the X axis), or `y' (only the Y axis).
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for grid formatting.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.linspace(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.sin(x)}

\CommentTok{\# Creating the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(x, y, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Adding the grid}
\NormalTok{plt.grid(}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, which}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}both\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Adding a title and axis labels}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Plot of the function sin(x)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}x\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sin(x)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# Displaying the chart}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\chapter{Matplotlib - other charts}\label{matplotlib---other-charts}

\section{Dual-axis chart}\label{dual-axis-chart}

The \texttt{twinx} function in the Matplotlib library allows you to
create a second Y axis that will share the X axis with the first Y axis.
Thanks to this, you can easily present two data series that are measured
in different units but have a common independent variable.

The function syntax is \texttt{twinx(ax=None,\ **kwargs)}, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{ax} - the Axes object to be used to create the new Y axis. Set
  to \texttt{None} by default, which means a new Y axis will be created.
\item
  \texttt{**kwargs} - additional arguments for formatting the new Y
  axis.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{fig, ax1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots()}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\FloatTok{0.01}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{10.0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.01}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ x }\OperatorTok{**} \DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{ax1.plot(x, y, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ax1.twinx()}
\NormalTok{y2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.sin(x)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(x, y2)}
\NormalTok{fig.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{matplotlib-othercharts_files/figure-pdf/cell-2-output-1.pdf}}

\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\NormalTok{fig, ax1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots()}
\NormalTok{t }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.arange(}\FloatTok{0.01}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{10.0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.01}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{s1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.exp(t)}
\NormalTok{ax1.plot(t, s1, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.set\_xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}time (s)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{ax1.set\_ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}exp\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.tick\_params(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, colors}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{ax2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ax1.twinx()}
\NormalTok{s2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.sin(}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ t)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(t, s2, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r.\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.set\_ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sin\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.tick\_params(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}y\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, colors}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{fig.tight\_layout()}
\NormalTok{plt.show(block}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
